    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer394427471" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13052969987251722772">La Familia de pascual Duarte = ‚ÄéThe Family of Pascual Duarte‚Ä¨, Camilo Jos√© Cela<br/><br/>The Family of Pascual Duarte is a 1942 novel written by Spanish Nobel laureate Camilo Jos√© Cela.<br/><br/>The novel is in fact considered the first novel of this style of writing, but also contains themes of extreme realism and existentialism: the characters live in the margins of society and their lives are submersed in anguish and pain; the archetype of this theme is found in the protagonist of the novel, Pascual Duarte, who </span>
  <span id="freeText13052969987251722772" style="display:none">La Familia de pascual Duarte = ‚ÄéThe Family of Pascual Duarte‚Ä¨, Camilo Jos√© Cela<br /><br />The Family of Pascual Duarte is a 1942 novel written by Spanish Nobel laureate Camilo Jos√© Cela.<br /><br />The novel is in fact considered the first novel of this style of writing, but also contains themes of extreme realism and existentialism: the characters live in the margins of society and their lives are submersed in anguish and pain; the archetype of this theme is found in the protagonist of the novel, Pascual Duarte, who has learned that violence is the only way to solve his problems. <br /><br />The Family of Pascual Duarte has various narrators, the main being Duarte, who recounts his history in a rural dialect.<br /><br />ÿ™ÿßÿ±€åÿÆ ŸÜÿÆÿ≥ÿ™€åŸÜ ÿÆŸàÿßŸÜÿ¥: ÿ±Ÿàÿ≤ ⁄ÜŸáÿßÿ±ŸÖ ŸÖÿßŸá ŸÖ€å ÿ≥ÿßŸÑ 2008 ŸÖ€åŸÑÿßÿØ€å<br /><br />ÿπŸÜŸàÿßŸÜ: ÿÆÿßŸÜŸàÿßÿØŸá Ÿæÿßÿ≥⁄©ŸàÿßŸÑ ÿØŸàÿ¢ÿ±ÿ™Ÿáÿõ ŸÜŸà€åÿ≥ŸÜÿØŸá: ⁄©ÿßŸÖ€åŸÑ€åÿß ÿÆŸàÿ≥Ÿá ÿ≥ŸÑÿßÿõ ŸÖÿ™ÿ±ÿ¨ŸÖ: ÿ≠ÿ≥ŸÜ Ÿæÿ≥ÿ™ÿßÿõ ÿ¥€åÿ±ÿßÿ≤ÿå ŸÜÿ¥ÿ± ÿ¥€åŸàÿßÿå 1369ÿå ÿØÿ± 167ÿµ<br /><br />ÿπŸÜŸàÿßŸÜ: ÿÆÿßŸÜŸàÿßÿØŸá Ÿæÿßÿ≥⁄©ŸàÿßŸÑ ÿØŸàÿ¢ÿ±ÿ™Ÿáÿõ ŸÜŸà€åÿ≥ŸÜÿØŸá: ⁄©ÿßŸÖ€åŸÑ€åÿß ÿÆŸàÿ≥Ÿá ÿ≥ŸÑÿßÿõ ŸÖÿ™ÿ±ÿ¨ŸÖ: ÿ≠ÿ≥ŸÜ Ÿæÿ≥ÿ™ÿßÿõ ÿ™Ÿáÿ±ÿßŸÜÿå ÿØŸÅÿ™ÿ±Ÿáÿß€å ÿ≤ŸÖÿßŸÜŸáÿå 1369ÿå ÿØÿ± 167ÿµ<br /><br />ÿπŸÜŸàÿßŸÜ: ÿÆÿßŸÜŸàÿßÿØŸá Ÿæÿßÿ≥⁄©ŸàÿßŸÑ ÿØŸàÿ¢ÿ±ÿ™Ÿáÿõ ŸÜŸà€åÿ≥ŸÜÿØŸá: ⁄©ÿßŸÖ€åŸÑ€åÿß ÿÆŸàÿ≥Ÿá ÿ≥ŸÑÿßÿõ ŸÖÿ™ÿ±ÿ¨ŸÖ: ŸÅÿ±ŸáÿßÿØ ÿ∫ÿ®ÿ±ÿß€å€åÿõ ÿ™Ÿáÿ±ÿßŸÜÿå ŸÖÿßŸá€åÿå 1986ÿõ ÿØÿ± 193ÿµÿõ ⁄ÜÿßŸæ ÿØŸàŸÖ 1388ÿõ ÿ¥ÿßÿ®⁄© 9789649971674ÿõ ⁄ÜÿßŸæ ÿ≥ŸàŸÖ 1391ÿõ ⁄ÜÿßŸæ ⁄ÜŸáÿßÿ±ŸÖ 1392ÿõ ⁄ÜÿßŸæ ŸæŸÜÿ¨ŸÖ 1395ÿõ ⁄ÜÿßŸæ ŸáŸÅÿ™ŸÖ 1397ÿõ ŸÖŸàÿ∂Ÿàÿπ: ÿØÿßÿ≥ÿ™ÿßŸÜŸáÿß€å ŸÜŸà€åÿ≥ŸÜÿØ⁄ØÿßŸÜ ÿßÿ≥ŸæÿßŸÜ€åÿßÿ¶€å - ÿ≥ÿØŸá 20ŸÖ<br /><br />⁄©ÿ™ÿßÿ® ¬´ÿÆÿßŸÜŸàÿßÿØŸá‚Äå €å Ÿæÿßÿ≥⁄©Ÿàÿ¢ŸÑ ÿØŸàÿ¢ÿ±ÿ™Ÿá¬ªÿå ÿßÿ´ÿ±: ¬´⁄©ÿßŸÖ€åŸÑŸà ÿÆŸàÿ≥Ÿá ÿ≥ŸêŸÑÿß¬ªÿå ÿ±ŸÖÿßŸÜ‚ÄåŸÜŸà€åÿ≥ Ÿà ÿ¥ÿßÿπÿ± ÿßÿ≥ŸæÿßŸÜ€åÿß€å€å ÿßÿ≥ÿ™ÿå ⁄©Ÿá ÿß€åÿ¥ÿßŸÜ ÿ±ÿß ÿØÿ± ÿ±ÿØ€åŸÅ ŸÜŸà€åÿ≥ŸÜÿØ⁄ØÿßŸÜ€å ŸáŸÖ⁄ÜŸàŸÜ ¬´ÿ≥ŸÑ€åŸÜ¬ª Ÿà ¬´ŸÖÿßŸÑÿß Ÿæÿßÿ±ÿ™Ÿá¬ª ŸÇÿ±ÿßÿ± ÿØÿßÿØŸá‚Äå ÿßŸÜÿØÿõ ⁄©ÿ™ÿßÿ® ¬´ÿÆÿßŸÜŸàÿßÿØŸá‚Äå €å Ÿæÿßÿ≥⁄©Ÿàÿ¢ŸÑ ÿØŸàÿ¢ÿ±ÿ™Ÿá¬ª ÿ±ÿß ŸÜ€åÿ≤ ŸáŸÖ‚Äåÿ≥ŸÜ⁄Ø ¬´ÿ®€å⁄ØÿßŸÜŸá¬ª ÿßÿ´ÿ± ¬´⁄©ÿßŸÖŸà¬ª ÿØÿßŸÜÿ≥ÿ™Ÿá‚Äå ÿßŸÜÿØÿõ ¬´Ÿæÿßÿ≥⁄©ŸàÿßŸÑ¬ª ŸáŸÖ ŸáŸÖÿßŸÜŸÜÿØ ¬´ŸÖŸàÿ±ÿ≥Ÿà¬ª€å ¬´ÿ®€å⁄ØÿßŸÜŸá¬ªÿå ŸÖÿ±ÿ™⁄©ÿ® ŸÇÿ™ŸÑ€å ÿ®€å‚ÄåŸáÿØŸÅ ŸÖ€å‚Äåÿ¥ŸàÿØÿõ ¬´ÿÆÿßŸÜŸàÿßÿØŸá‚Äå €å Ÿæÿßÿ≥⁄©Ÿàÿ¢ŸÑ ÿØŸàÿ¢ÿ±ÿ™Ÿá¬ª ÿ®ÿ±ÿ±ÿ≥€å ÿ±ŸàÿßŸÜ⁄©ÿßŸàÿßŸÜŸá €å ÿ™ÿ±ÿ≥ ÿßÿ≥ÿ™ÿõ ÿÆÿ¥ŸàŸÜÿ™ÿå ÿ™ÿ±ÿ≥ÿå Ÿà ÿßÿ≠ÿ≥ÿßÿ≥ ⁄ØŸÜÿßŸáÿå ŸÜÿ≥ÿ®ÿ™ ÿ®Ÿá ÿ¢ŸÜ. ⁄Ü⁄ØŸàŸÜŸá ÿßŸÜÿ≥ÿßŸÜ€å ⁄©Ÿá Ÿæÿß⁄© ÿßÿ≤ ŸÖÿßÿØÿ± ÿ≤ÿßÿØŸá ÿ¥ÿØŸá ÿÆŸàÿØÿ¢⁄ØÿßŸá €åÿß ŸÜÿßÿÆŸàÿØÿ¢⁄ØÿßŸá ÿ®Ÿá ÿß€åŸÜ ÿØÿ±ÿ¨Ÿá ÿßÿ≤ ÿ™Ÿá€å ÿ®ŸàÿØŸÜ ÿßÿ≤ ÿßŸÜÿ≥ÿßŸÜ€åÿ™ ŸÖ€å‚Äåÿ±ÿ≥ÿØÿü<br /><br />ÿ™ÿßÿ±€åÿÆ ÿ®ŸáŸÜ⁄ØÿßŸÖ ÿ±ÿ≥ÿßŸÜ€å 11/04/1399Ÿáÿ¨ÿ±€å ÿÆŸàÿ±ÿ¥€åÿØ€åÿõ ÿß. ÿ¥ÿ±ÿ®€åÿßŸÜ€å</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer514226983" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer16987613654976778187">For fans of Spanish miserablism set in a heartless deterministic universe (i.e. this one), <i>Pascal Duarte</i> is the brief novel for you. Duarte‚Äôs confession, written from prison, is a beautiful recounting of a life of violent poverty and aimless murder, told in simple and frequently moving prose. Cela‚Äôs work is often concerned with the seemingly endless human capacity for violence and conflict and this short work leaves a powerful imprint on the reader with its moments of hair-raising cruelty and al</span>
  <span id="freeText16987613654976778187" style="display:none">For fans of Spanish miserablism set in a heartless deterministic universe (i.e. this one), <i>Pascal Duarte</i> is the brief novel for you. Duarte‚Äôs confession, written from prison, is a beautiful recounting of a life of violent poverty and aimless murder, told in simple and frequently moving prose. Cela‚Äôs work is often concerned with the seemingly endless human capacity for violence and conflict and this short work leaves a powerful imprint on the reader with its moments of hair-raising cruelty and almost unbearable tenderness.</span>

<div id="review_669452502" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/669452502" />
    <a title="Vanessa J." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19590155-vanessa-j"><img alt="Vanessa J." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1468102148p2/19590155.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/669452502?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vanessa J." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vanessa J." href="/user/show/19590155-vanessa-j">Vanessa J.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=2013">2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=humans-are-scary">humans-are-scary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=philosophical">philosophical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=dark-as-my-soul">dark-as-my-soul</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=mwahaha-gore">mwahaha-gore</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=psychological">psychological</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=spain">spain</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=unreliable-narrator">unreliable-narrator</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer669452502" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17096262734984431619"><blockquote>
  <i>I am not, sir, a bad person, though in all truth I am not lacking in reason for being one.</i>
</blockquote><br/><br/>Pascual Duarte has done many things in his life. Some of them took him to prison not once but <i>twice</i> times. He's now under his second sentence and he's about to be executed. Thus, he wrote a diary in which he told his entire life and the people who influenced it.<br/><br/>He killed many people. He states that at the beginning, so don't you worry about spoilers. Some in cold blood, some because of revenge, others b</span>
  <span id="freeText17096262734984431619" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>I am not, sir, a bad person, though in all truth I am not lacking in reason for being one.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Pascual Duarte has done many things in his life. Some of them took him to prison not once but <i>twice</i> times. He's now under his second sentence and he's about to be executed. Thus, he wrote a diary in which he told his entire life and the people who influenced it.<br /><br />He killed many people. He states that at the beginning, so don't you worry about spoilers. Some in cold blood, some because of revenge, others because of self-defense, etc. The thing is, the man was a murderer.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>You kill without thinking, I have well tested; sometimes unintentionally. You hate intensely, fiercely, and the blade is opened and with it wide open, barefoot, you come to the bed where the enemy sleeps.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />His past is dark and terrible. I wouldn't wish anyone a life like the one he had. His father and mother abused him, his sister escaped from the house at 16 because she couldn't bear living there any more, the man who dishonors his sister ends up sleeping with his first wife and gets her pregnant.<br /><br />Plus, he was violent by nature, so should you be surprised about the things he's yet capable of doing? <b>Yes, you should.</b><br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>The truth is that life in my family had little to recommend. But since we are not given a choice, but rather are destined‚Äîeven from before birth‚Äîto go some of us one way, some the other, I did my best to accept my fate, which was the only way to avoid desperation.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />You should not be surprised that the general tone in this book is pessimistic. I don't have a problem with that, in case you were wondering. For some reason still unknown to me, these kind of books end up being my favorite.<br /><br />The realism of the novel is also overwhelming and good. The situations portrayed in the book are things that still happen, and since it's narrated in 1st person POV, you can see Pascual's opinions on them. And also, you get the opportunity of being inside a murderer's head.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>I'm not made to philosophize, I don't have the heart for it. My heart is more like a machine for making blood to be spilled in a knife fight...</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Many people would say this man was mad, but was he indeed? That's actually a rather difficult question for me to answer. When I read this in 2013, my Spanish teacher asked us that question. I've been thinking since that moment if the man was crazy or not, and I still cannot come up with an answer.<br /><br />Also, is he a bad person? He states at the beginning that he is not, but that he has many reasons to be so. Certainly sometimes he's not acting according to the moral, and his motives may not justify his actions, yet he still claims he's neither a mad nor a bad person.<br /><br />The ending of the book is also open for interpretations. Was Pascual trying to redeem himself? Was he simply telling his life because he was proud of his actions? Was he just merely trying to explain things?<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>I wanted to put ground between my shadow and myself, between my name and me, between the memory of my name and the rest of me, between my flesh and me myself, that me myself who, without shadow and name and memory and flesh would be almost nothing.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />This is the thing with unreliable narrators: You can never trust all the things they say. Yes, there are some facts about his life that he can't modify because they're things everyone knew... but what about the feelings? What about his inner thoughts? Should they be trusted too? Not in the majority of the cases.<br /><br />The letters at the beginning and the ending of the book are the only clues as to what you should think about his diary, because the person writing the letters is just as puzzled as the reader.<br /><br />Anyway, I recommend this. I'm not really sure about this book being one of my all-time-favorites, but it sure was good enough to pass the 2015 re-read test.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17096262734984431619" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15559428256483960366">This one is morbidly intriguing. It's sick fun to get into Pascual's head, "con perd√≥n". Pascual kills his dog, his rival, his mom, your mom, and damned if you don't feel sorry for him in spite of it all. There is nothing sadder than a baby (Pascual's little neglected brother) having his ears eaten by a pig. Stop laughing, this is sad stuff.</span>

<div id="review_1194573749" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1194573749" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18352095-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363353243p2/18352095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1194573749?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/18352095-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" " href="/book/show/7556457-la-familia-de-pascual-duarte">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18352095-teresa?shelf=2015-re-reads">2015-re-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18352095-teresa?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18352095-teresa?shelf=popsugar-2015-book-challenge">popsugar-2015-book-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1194573749" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17909224069268351743"><br/>I am rating this highly praised classic in the Spanish literature with just one star and if I could have, I'd given it 0. I am not basing my rating on its literary merit: the presumed originality, the social commentary value, the poverty and ignorance in rural Spain in the pre-war years, the Nobel prize, yada, yada,yada. <br/>I am just rating it so low because I hated it. <br/><br/>So, yes. I have detested this book from beginning to end. It has been claimed as one of the best novels in the Spanish language, a</span>
  <span id="freeText17909224069268351743" style="display:none"><br />I am rating this highly praised classic in the Spanish literature with just one star and if I could have, I'd given it 0. I am not basing my rating on its literary merit: the presumed originality, the social commentary value, the poverty and ignorance in rural Spain in the pre-war years, the Nobel prize, yada, yada,yada. <br />I am just rating it so low because I hated it. <br /><br />So, yes. I have detested this book from beginning to end. It has been claimed as one of the best novels in the Spanish language, and it was written by a Nobel laureate that incidentally, I also detested when he was alive. He was a rude, arrogant and belligerent polemicist who enjoyed insulting other people from his TV platform. To me, he was, after all, a fascist. Now, trying to be honest, could it be that I was truly biased against the book even before I started it? Mmmmm, not sure, really. Could be! *Shrugs* <br /><br />The Family of Pascual Duarte is the story of a psychopathic serial killer, portrayed in the form of a memoir that he writes from death row and then sends to a friend of one of the people he killed. I also contemplated the possibility that he was actually schizophrenic, and therefore of diminished moral responsibility, but there is actually little evidence that this is the case, so to me he is simply evil. <br /><br />I have found the theme and the setting utterly depressing, an example of the particular brand of truculent Spanish realism termed "miserabilism" or "tremendismo". In this way, it has been compared with Sartre's and Camus's existentialism, but I do not really see this so clearly, as while I can acknowledge the existential strands in the book, (probably the deepest one surely is the question about the ontology of evil, i.e. is evil innate or learned through adverse life experiences?), both Sartre and Camus are among my favourite authors and I clearly remember respecting them in my teens, when I read their books, and not only due to their political ideology, which is diametrally opposed to Cela's. On the contrary, I have always despised Cela, and unfortunately I cannot think I can do a good job of separating Cela the man, and Cela the author. <br /><br />I will really not get into the content of the book too much, but I'd just like to discuss the form briefly. I have read the original Spanish version and I have found the language archaic and difficult to follow. I have even needed to keep the dictionary near me while I was reading, despite Spanish being my mother tongue. This does not make enjoyable reading, but I'll admit that being written in 1942 and centring in a rural location, and with a peasant protagonist, it is probably unavoidable that it would contain many words currently abandoned. The style is florid and verbose, and the text is peppered with glaring grammatical mistakes that distracted me a lot from the narrative. I can see that these mistakes might have been completely deliberate by the author (who was, after all, a member of the Royal Academy of the Spanish Language) when attempting to evoke the idea of a semi-illiterate rural labourer writing his memoirs, but I have not enjoyed reading this and I think that the effect is not fully realised. The narrative still feels jarring and the idea of Pascual, who only had a very few years of schooling writing this is not credible. <br /><br />While being immersed in worlds far away in time, distance or imagination from the world I live in generally brings me pleasure, this particular universe only brought me pain. And pain is perhaps the most central theme of the book. Deeply rooted, constant existential pain. There is misery everywhere, in every paragraph and every page. Complete ignorance, abject poverty and spiritual misery in every nook and cranny of the souls of most of the characters, not only Pascual. It is a terrible book steeped in violence, evil and misery. Pascual is an animal, and he feels no remorse for his criminal deeds. He does talk about repenting from his sins, but this is mere egocentric self-pity, not true grief and repentance coming from a well developed theory of mind and from the capacity of placing himself in the shoes of those he killed. <br /><br />In have found this very short, very annoying book a total bore. Oh, well, it was another of the pesky assignments for my reading challenge this year, which so far is proving, errh, precisely that: challenging.<br /><br /><br />Popsugar Reading Challenge 2015. #25. A book you were supposed to read in High School but didn't.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17909224069268351743" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_62122076" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/62122076" />
    <a title="jeremy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/635936-jeremy"><img alt="jeremy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569705896p2/635936.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/62122076?book_show_action=true">Jul 04, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="jeremy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="jeremy" href="/user/show/635936-jeremy">jeremy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/635936-jeremy?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/635936-jeremy?shelf=translation">translation</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer62122076" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1104523673299109438">camilo jos√© cela, spain's fifth nobel laureate, is perhaps best known for two novels, <i>the hive (la colmena)</i> and <i>the family of pascual duarte (la familia de pascual duarte)</i>. the latter of these is a magnificent work of fiction he composed when he was only 26 years old. <i>the family of pascual duarte</i> relates the story of a castilian peasant serving a prison term for multiple murders. <br/><br/>the story is told from the perspective of duarte himself, as he spent his time incarcerated writing his life story an</span>
  <span id="freeText1104523673299109438" style="display:none">camilo jos√© cela, spain's fifth nobel laureate, is perhaps best known for two novels, <i>the hive (la colmena)</i> and <i>the family of pascual duarte (la familia de pascual duarte)</i>. the latter of these is a magnificent work of fiction he composed when he was only 26 years old. <i>the family of pascual duarte</i> relates the story of a castilian peasant serving a prison term for multiple murders. <br /><br />the story is told from the perspective of duarte himself, as he spent his time incarcerated writing his life story and recalling what led him to commit these heinous crimes. at the beginning and ending of the novel, cela includes letters from the transcriber of duarte's memoir and the prison chaplain (amongst others), explaining more about duarte's character and how his papers came to be published. while the novel is fantastically composed, these extra literary stylings add a certain charm and authenticity to the story. <br /><br />while pascal duarte had killed with little remorse, cela is able to convey duarte's humanity in such a convincing manner that one cannot help but have sympathy for the convicted murderer. cela portrays duarte (or rather, duarte, thus, portrays himself) as having endured many hardships, some unimaginably debilitating, and while we are not expected to condone the homicides, we can almost understand the mindset and emotional state that led up to his reprehensible acts. it is no small feat of craft that cela was able to create a main character whose actions are entirely deplorable, yet depict him in such a way that it stirs compassion in the reader. <i>the family of pascual duarte</i> is a short, yet riveting tale, almost flawlessly composed, rich with insights into human frailty and desperation. it is a truly stunning read.<br /><br /><br />"the inhabitants of cities live with their backs to the truth, and oftentimes they are not even aware that only a couple of leagues away, in the middle of the plain, a country man may be thinking about <i>them</i> while he packs up his gear, folds his fishing rod and picks up his little wicker basket with its six or seven eels inside."</span>
  <a data-text-id="1104523673299109438" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13650725171661257688" style="display:none">Can a person fall in love with a book? <br />I did. Twice.<br />However, I have this problem. Every time I like someone/something I convince myself I DO NOT. Probably because of some fucked up ideals I built in my head since childhood. <br />So this is me, pretending I didn‚Äôt fall for Jose‚Äôs dedication ‚ÄúI dedicate this edition to my enemies, who have helped me so much in my career‚Äù or Pascual‚Äôs first sentence ‚ÄúI am not sir, a bad person, though in all truth I am not lacking in reasons for being one.‚Äù<br />This is me telling myself the prose was pessimistic with an agonizing philosophy which cannot be real; it‚Äôs a fatality we only find in books. Mother Nature and Father God treat us better. <br />This is me attempting to loathe Duarte because of his nature which is so like ours but still remaining doubtful in front of that Hamletian question; are we born evil or do we become one through life? <br />This is me defining sin satanic, merely because I live by society‚Äôs concepts. What if our ideas of values and truth are simply a mirage, shaped from what we think is right? <br />This is me, putting up my head a shiny aura which lightens those dark raw desires of being owned in and out by another human‚Äôs flesh. <br />This is me believing killing is not easy like Duarte thinks, me walking head high without counting every dead organism under my feet. <br />I guess this is me trying to hate the book for its horrendous, atrocious translation (specially the dialogues); me disappointed because I could never despise Duarte strongly enough, me fearing the silhouette of someone I could become, me dreading my end‚Ä¶<br /></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2308235369?book_show_action=true">Nov 26, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2308235369" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9048720085050447268">Published in 1942, the same year as <i> Camus‚Äôs The Stranger </i> this short novel is the dark story of Pascual Duarte, a brutal Spanish peasant, whose life has been shaped by poverty, ignorance and hatred. The story recounts his mounting depravity as he goes from killing his dog to knifing a romantic rival to final horrific matricide. <br/>It‚Äôs a raw and compelling read that has surprisingly been out of print as a translated version since not long after it won the Nobel Prize in 1989. </span>

<div id="review_3351961252" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3351961252" />
    <a title="Alex Tully" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34745281-alex-tully"><img alt="Alex Tully" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3351961252?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex Tully" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex Tully" href="/user/show/34745281-alex-tully">Alex Tully</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3351961252" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11983560795630299085"><br/>Having been written a short time after this novel‚Äôs completion, this review is perhaps lacking a certain fervour associated with the immediate afterthought of such a riveting read.<br/><br/>Nevertheless, The Family of Pascual Duarte was simply superb. One of my more fruitful crepuscular ventures, coming across Cela amidst a tedious search of Nobel Laureates proved to be an extremely astute discovery. Lacking behind numerous other countries with regard to the literary impact the Spanish script has on Briti</span>
  <span id="freeText11983560795630299085" style="display:none"><br />Having been written a short time after this novel‚Äôs completion, this review is perhaps lacking a certain fervour associated with the immediate afterthought of such a riveting read.<br /><br />Nevertheless, The Family of Pascual Duarte was simply superb. One of my more fruitful crepuscular ventures, coming across Cela amidst a tedious search of Nobel Laureates proved to be an extremely astute discovery. Lacking behind numerous other countries with regard to the literary impact the Spanish script has on British readers (owing in part to Franco‚Äôs tirade against the Arts), there is a distinct feeling of venturing into the unknown when delving into the Castilian rabbithole - Don Quixote and some works of Marais aside. <br /><br />Far from the romanticised idyll one associates with Spain, Cela details his native land warts and all. This is portrayed with the novel nearly exclusively taking in the autonomous community of Extremadura, one of Spain‚Äôs poorest regions and one most will not know, bordering Portugal to the West. The narrative takes place in a harsh unfiltered rural village: the closest major city of the area being Merida. What Cela does so well through superb descriptive prose is the depiction of a feeling of hopelessness and poverty throughout. Considering the seemingly endless occurrences of appalling acts may not necessarily appear impressive, they are when carefully scrutinised. This is to say, only when these acts are objectively viewed as enriching and potentially happy, do you see a true master of his craft. An outstanding example is the description of the eponymous character‚Äôs wedding. Cela describes the day in a laboured and disinterested manner, as if he himself is forcing the words onto the page. The way the wedding culminates in an impromptu drunken duel typifies the brutal effect of Cela‚Äôs dose of realism.<br /><br />Due to the nature of the novel - a character who has committed heinous crimes, reciting the moments that led him to them - as well as the publishing date of 1942, comparisons have been made to the more recognised and renowned ‚ÄòThe Stranger‚Äô by French author Albert Camus. Personally, this is nothing more than a comparison of convenience and is ultimately a lazy and futile one. Not only is Cela‚Äôs the more objectively impressive, but the psychology of the two main characters differs greatly, and thus the motivation for their respective attacks is completely different. One is essentially borne out of apathy, the other misfortune and poverty. The tale is completely in keeping with other ‚ÄòGeneration of 36‚Äô novelists, instantaneously comparable to Beliebes‚Äô ‚ÄòLas Ratas‚Äô as a prime example of the morbidity and sense of desperation found in rural, post-Civil War Spain. <br /><br />Whilst this review so far has centered on a more abstract scrutinisation, my concluding paragraph briefly gives synopsis, adding more direct information and context behind the novel. As the title suggests, all events are intrinsically linked to the anti-hero‚Äôs tumultuous relationship with his family. Duarte‚Äôs father, a Portuguese brute, succumbs to rabies early on. Yet, it is clearly Cela‚Äôs foreshadowing about Duarte‚Äôs mother that is more appetising for the reader. One passage in particular draws attention, in which the pustules surrounding the mother‚Äôs mouth are denoted as changing seasonally, projecting a more morbid outward appearance throughout the Summer months. This explicit display of grotesqueness ties in beautifully with the pre-existing descriptions of the ramshackled house the family inhabit, the bleakness and lack of prosperity of the immediate vicinity. The fact that Duarte‚Äôs bizarre but fundamentally loving relationship with his sister, seemingly the only positive relationship in the entire novel, is constantly smited by cutbacks and poor timing is arguably the most tragic aspect of the main character‚Äôs descent into madness. Who is to say in an alternative universe she could have provided him with the stability and willpower that would‚Äôve curtailed his psychosis and bloodlust? <br /><br />In conclusion, Cela‚Äôs novel is a grim and harrowing - yet also thought-provoking - novel. It is one that I would recommend to anyone with a hint of interest in the fundementalist and gritty cultural phenomena of rural Spain: a land often overlooked, yet written about with a clear sense of nihilistic realism and thinly-veiled critique towards the direction in which Camilo Jos√© Cela saw his great country going. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11983560795630299085" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2604144594" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2604144594" />
    <a title="Iulia Marin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34731539-iulia-marin"><img alt="Iulia Marin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1481735128p2/34731539.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2604144594?book_show_action=true">Nov 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Iulia Marin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Iulia Marin" href="/user/show/34731539-iulia-marin">Iulia Marin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2604144594" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2450535656065544166">lthough he writes in the nihilist mode, Cela, unlike most modern practitioners, starts from no preconceived positions; his premises tend to evolve as he goes along and then just as easily disappear all together. Is Pascual a cautionary tale, a psychological study, or mere naturalism of a dark, distressing sort? One never knows. All these ingredients are there, but brand names seem irrelevant. Cela's anti-hero is a man whose humanity is close to that of an animal, and yet what he has to say of hi</span>
  <span id="freeText2450535656065544166" style="display:none">lthough he writes in the nihilist mode, Cela, unlike most modern practitioners, starts from no preconceived positions; his premises tend to evolve as he goes along and then just as easily disappear all together. Is Pascual a cautionary tale, a psychological study, or mere naturalism of a dark, distressing sort? One never knows. All these ingredients are there, but brand names seem irrelevant. Cela's anti-hero is a man whose humanity is close to that of an animal, and yet what he has to say of himself (it's in the first person) is so blunt and blunderingly honest that a bleak pathos is evoked on every page. Outwardly he's a proletarian figure living in the sticks north of Madrid in the '20's. Grown up without love (a drunken father, stone-faced mother), as a field hand he feels nothing in nature. He has a sister-fixation but she's little more than a whore. Everything he touches falls apart. He marries, his children die; his first wife's unfaithful, his second can't help. Early on in the course of events his impulses turn inwards. He's deemed to commit one senseless murder after another, beginning with the shooting of his pet dog, ending with the horrendous- and that is the right word- stabbing of his mother. What is one to make of all this? Zola's La Bete Humaine used similar melodramatics, but there it was a sort of mental hygiene appeal. Cela's world is as bonebare as a geometric proposition and as inexorable. Though its style and language suits the class of its narrator and though not a bit of pity seeps through, something close to tragedy, close to a catharsis is felt. A strange achievement; the most disturbing and disturbingly good Spanish writing since Borges, whose nerve-wracking ceremoniousness is the underside of Cela's stark, spiney neutrality.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2450535656065544166" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15667216935627887825">This book was a pleasant surprise. The story is often horrifying and gruesome, but the character of Pascual Duarte cannot said to be coldblooded, no matter the violence he inflicts upon others. Because he starts by telling his background story, you understand the motives of his crimes. The strength of the novel lies in Pascual's humaneness and the different perspectives that are offered by the testimonies at the end.</span>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/113324-robin?shelf=obscure">obscure</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer204921611" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16896451707273705555">Well I mostly rated this book as 3 stars because there were parts that I couldn't follow what was happening. Perhaps that was intentional, perhaps it was lost in translation, but it was frustrating to be turning pages back and forth going, "wait, what? did he kill that guy? did he kill his wife? oh, he just left the city. I guess?" etc. <br/><br/>I'm surprised when people don't like a book because of a narrator that is evil or unlikable. I really enjoy being forced to be "on the side" of the narrator that</span>
  <span id="freeText16896451707273705555" style="display:none">Well I mostly rated this book as 3 stars because there were parts that I couldn't follow what was happening. Perhaps that was intentional, perhaps it was lost in translation, but it was frustrating to be turning pages back and forth going, "wait, what? did he kill that guy? did he kill his wife? oh, he just left the city. I guess?" etc. <br /><br />I'm surprised when people don't like a book because of a narrator that is evil or unlikable. I really enjoy being forced to be "on the side" of the narrator that cannot be trusted. Similar to the narrators in Lolita or The Collector. It makes you constantly skeptical of the narrator's version of events which is a neat literary device. I also enjoyed the similarity to "The Stranger": in both books the narrator stresses that he had no choice, and anyway it was all fate/circumstance pushing him into his misdeeds. The appeal to the reader that an injustice is being done to the narrator because they are being punished for their crime, a crime they HAD to commit.<br /><br />So, overall, good, not great. I'm glad I randomly found it in a used bookstore.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16896451707273705555" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15837399035781462382">I'd only recommend this novel if you're interested in the work that started Nobel Prize winner Camilo Jos√© Cela's career in 1942, or if you enjoy a matter-of-fact prose style, reminiscent of the style of New Objectivity. It's a short book with a neat framing device and a somewhat open ending, which I liked. But I just couldn't relate to anyone in this novel, least of all Duarte, who is narrating this story as he is sitting in his prison cell, about to be executed for murder. Characters don't hav</span>
  <span id="freeText15837399035781462382" style="display:none">I'd only recommend this novel if you're interested in the work that started Nobel Prize winner Camilo Jos√© Cela's career in 1942, or if you enjoy a matter-of-fact prose style, reminiscent of the style of New Objectivity. It's a short book with a neat framing device and a somewhat open ending, which I liked. But I just couldn't relate to anyone in this novel, least of all Duarte, who is narrating this story as he is sitting in his prison cell, about to be executed for murder. Characters don't have to be likable, but we do have to recognize something relatable and human in them to understand their actions and looking at other reviews on goodreads, that seems to be the case for most readers. Not for me, unfortunately. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15837399035781462382" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_50653780" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/50653780" />
    <a title="Stephen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/754325-stephen"><img alt="Stephen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1467223050p2/754325.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/50653780?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen" href="/user/show/754325-stephen">Stephen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer50653780&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50653780" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18055830417470187600"><i>This review first appeared in "Margin" which is an online literary publication focusing on magical realism edited by Tamara Kaye Sellman</i>.<br/><br/><br/>IN CAMILO Jos√© Cela‚Äôs The Family of Pascual Duarte, people, plants, animals and other natural forces take on shimmering qualities when a murderous madman projects his imagination over a gray and barren landscape. <br/><br/>His nameless and impoverished agrarian village is located, Pascual tells us, "some two leagues from Almendralejo, squatting athwart a road as empty an</span>
  <span id="freeText18055830417470187600" style="display:none"><i>This review first appeared in "Margin" which is an online literary publication focusing on magical realism edited by Tamara Kaye Sellman</i>.<br /><br /><br />IN CAMILO Jos√© Cela‚Äôs The Family of Pascual Duarte, people, plants, animals and other natural forces take on shimmering qualities when a murderous madman projects his imagination over a gray and barren landscape. <br /><br />His nameless and impoverished agrarian village is located, Pascual tells us, "some two leagues from Almendralejo, squatting athwart a road as empty and endless as a day without bread..." <br /><br />He works only rarely, and his account reads like one of a low-rent bon vivant flitting about indulging self-generated paranoias and fears that might hold less sway were he out tending fields with more consistency. <br /><br />His personal poverty is a relative thing. He enjoys a modicum of economic independence and the ability to make a pleasure trip with his bride to the provincial capital. The house he describes is clean and appointed for basic necessities, even if the family burro occupies the room adjacent. <br /><br />Yet, for all the poverty Pascual claims to suffer, he does not seek exculpation for his serial murders by invoking a drab past or rotten luck. More important than poverty in the formation and motivation of Pascual lurks the shadow of his religion. <br /><br />Compounding the grayness of the narrator‚Äôs environment are the proscriptions of Spanish Catholicism, more severe and reliant on penitence than its gentler, more charitable Italian cousin. <br />Spanish philosopher and author Miguel de Unamuno defined this social order as "One faith, one shepherd, one flock, unity before anything else, unity imposed from on-high, repose, submission and obedience." <br /><br />The Catholic and Castilian code, Unamuno wrote, implied two worlds: "A God and a devil over each, hell to fear and a heaven to conquer through liberty and grace, gaining a merciful and just God." <br /><br />Indeed, God and proper convention are never far from our murderer‚Äôs mind. After his mare kicks an old lady he stops to check on her "...for it would not be in the nature of a well-born person to ride on." <br /><br />Pascual's relationship to the Church marks the real boundaries to his actions and perceptions. Its laws lend an otherworldly allure to what they forbid. Eve, after all, was naked and she gave Adam an apple to eat, not a bar of soap. <br /><br />While visiting the local friar to discuss his intentions of marrying the village maiden Lola, "Don Manuel opened the door of the sacristy and pointed to a bench in church, a bench like any bench in any church, made of unpainted wood, hard and cold as stone, but a place where sometimes wonderful moments are possible." <br /><br />The Lord clearly taketh in Pascual‚Äôs life, but giveth on occasion as well. To the considerable extent that Pascual has faith in God, he has faith in the devil and the archangels and demons as a result. <br /><br />While out spending a placid day in the country with his hunting dog Chispa, the animal (he says) turns to gaze on him with "the look of a confessor, coldly scrutinizing, the eyes of a lynx, the look they say a lynx fixes on you." <br /><br />Pascual is unable to shake the resulting shudder that wracks his body and overcomes him. <br /><br />"It was hot, the heat was stifling, and my eyes began to close under the animal‚Äôs stare, which was sharp as flint. <br />"I picked up my gun and fired. I reloaded and fired again. The bitch‚Äôs blood was dark and sticky and it spread slowly along the dry earth." <br /><br />It appears, then, to be Pascual‚Äôs destiny to kill; to that end, his assignment becomes one he fulfills consistently. Along the way, he also slashes a man in a barroom brawl and stabs to death a mare that has thrown Lola and killed the baby she carried inside.<br /><br />Pascual becomes a fugutive for a number of years, but returns to kill his tormentor, Estirao (Stretch), who first abused his sister and later impregnated Lola in Pascual‚Äôs absence. <br /><br />Pascual eventually lands in jail, where the peasant from Extremadura (meaning "extreme" and "hard" in Spanish) pens his memoirs from death row. These memoirs constitute the story of Pascual Duarte throughout the majority of the book. <br /><br />No madman on a self-destructive binge, Pascual does manage to be released on good behavior. At that moment he begins, earnest as ever, to rebuild a life, this time marrying Esperanza. But his demons get the worst of him and his mother pays the ultimate price. <br /><br />Of course,the story is about Pascual‚Äôs family, the most important social unit in agrarian settings such as this. His father is Portugese and an explosive madman who has a heart attack at the news of being cuckolded. <br /><br />His sister Rosario, whom Pascual adores, is cursed with a similar, if less violent, destiny because "God did not wish any of us to be distinguished by good deeds..." She is a prostitute, which can be shameful and painful before the sacred community, not to mention fatal to her as a practitioner. <br /><br />His brother, Mario, sired by a man other than Pascual‚Äôs father, is born deformed. <br /><br />"The poor fellow never got beyond dragging himself along the floor as is he were a snake and making some squeaking sounds in his throat. It was all he ever learned." <br /><br />The unfortunate Mario even suffers the indignity of having a pig chew off his ears. Eventually he relieves the family of his oppressive sadness by drowning in a vat of olive oil at 10 years old: <br /><br />"When we lifted him out, a thin trickle of oil poured from his mouth, like a gold thread being unwound from a spool in his belly. His hair, which in life had always been the dim color of ash, shone with such lively luster that one would have thought it had resurrected in death. Such were the wonders associated with the death of little Mario."<br /><br />Pascual's mother is conniving and untrustworthy, giving birth to children not her husband‚Äôs and encouraging her daughter-in-law Lola to do the same. <br /><br />Back in that hornets‚Äô nest after his term in prison, Pascual threatens the old woman so that she removes herself as instigator, none of which escapes Pascual, who observes: "It‚Äôs sad to think that in order to gain a little peace a man has to make use of fear!" <br /><br />But fear is not retribution enough and, in the novel‚Äôs dramatic highpoint, Pascual kills his mother and records his first impressions..."Her blood spurted all over my face. It was warm as a soft belly and tasted like the blood of a lamb." <br /><br />True to form, Pascual flees the scene. At this point his personal narration ends and outside voices, introduced by Cela in the form of public testimony and private missive, fill in the rest. <br /><br />The dispassionate diaries Pascual pens to divulge his murders have prompted comparisons with Albert Camus‚Äô The Stranger. Both books were published in 1943; Cela‚Äôs went on to become the most polemic and most prolifically translated of Spain‚Äôs 20th Century literary output. Camus did as much for French letters. <br /><br />But Pascual is not empty of soul in the way of Camus‚Äô feckless anti-hero; rather he is driven by the customs and practices of a pervasive moral code. He fears the Holy Ghost. <br /><br />His narration of events is not relayed in any linear way because, as Pascual explains, "Following the footsteps of people involved rather than the order of events, I jump from beginning to end and from the end back to the beginning. Like a grasshopper being swatted." <br /><br />The memoirs tell his version of events, of a good man driven by intermittent and irrational forces to kill. Pascual admits it freely in his opening. <br /><br />"I am not, sir, a bad person, though in all truth I am not lacking in reasons for being one. We are all born naked, and yet, as we begin to grow up, it pleases Destiny to vary us, as if we were made of wax." <br /><br />Recollections from his confessor ("the transcriber" of his testimony introduced at the beginning of the book) and the warden make clear that, following Pascual's release from jail, the murderer had more killing to do. <br /><br />We learn that at the Spanish Civil War‚Äôs outset, Pascual engaged in "revolutionary activities" that led him to kill the richest man in the town. The memoirs were sent, at Pascual‚Äôs request, to the only friend of his victim "whose address he can remember." <br /><br />The date of Pascual's eventual execution makes it likely, and the book works to suggest, that he was not shot in the end for his serial murdering, but, ironically, for his politics. Posted to a moral social code, Pascual is ultimately killed by the forces of Generalissimo Francisco Franco, keepers of that very same code. <br /><br />Adding contrast to Pascual's atrocities are the peasant village life and style that unfold in what Unamuno called the "intra-historical" cycle of birth, marriage, procreation and death, or "the world of silences." <br />Pascual chooses a wife at his brother‚Äôs funeral: <br /><br />"When Lola went down on her knees she showed the smooth whiteness of her legs above her black stockings, tight as blood sausage. I blush to say what I must, and may God apply the effort it cost me to say it toward the salvation of my soul, for the truth is in that moment I was glad my brother had died...Lola‚Äôs legs shone like silverplate, the blood pounded in my temples, and my heart seemed ready to burst from my chest." <br /><br />Here, Cela is superimposing rituals of passage to accentuate the eventless existence of agrarian life. These rites provide the only signposts for direction and action in an otherwise mundane universe. <br />Pascual gains Lola, but the imprimatur of the church robs her of allure, for "that first kiss given with permission didn‚Äôt taste half as good as the kisses in the cemetery, so long ago now." <br /><br />Sexual pleasure and death are also coupled in the aforementioned stabbing of the mare. "When I quit the stable my arm was aching. I was covered with blood up to my elbow. The mare hadn‚Äôt made a sound. She only breathed deeper, and faster, like when we put her to stud." <br /><br />And the flow of blood is often swathed in an inviting metaphor of rejuvenation and cleansing: "When they carried him off to Don Raimundo‚Äôs pharmacy the blood was flowing from him like water from a spring..." <br /><br />Cela marks a life-rhythm in Pascual‚Äôs pueblo using a trance-like dirge from a single, mournful drum. "The years passed over our heads as they do all the world. Life in our house went down the same drains as always..." <br /><br />When Pascual's son dies, he is tortured by the endless chatter of the women in his family. <br /><br />" ‚ÄòOh, the agony, the death throes!‚Äô <br />‚ÄòI held him gasping in my arms!‚Äô [they cry:] <br /><br />It sounded like a litany, as slow and weary as a night filled with wine, as languid and heavy as the pace of an ass. And they went on in this way day after day, week after week...It was frightful, dreadful, and the curse of God, vengeance from on high." <br /><br />Pascual‚Äôs is the superstition of a provincial haunted by ill-fortune, relieved only by splashes of momentary magic. <br />Happy in a family life, he and Lola seemingly conjure the boy‚Äôs death, inviting an ill-wind that kills him: <br /><br />" ‚ÄòDid you hear that?‚Äô <br />‚ÄòWhat?‚Äô <br /><br />‚ÄòThe window.‚Äô <br /><br />‚ÄòThe window?‚Äô <br /><br />‚ÄòYes. It creaked as if the wind, as if a draft were trying to get through...‚Äô <br /><br />The creaking of the window, moved as it was by the wind. Came to be mingled with a moan." <br /><br />The modern mind scoffs at the individual‚Äôs dark power to conjure death, but Pascual‚Äôs mind does not. He leaves his future "in God‚Äôs hands" along with responsibility for his past transgressions, for the lamb of God takes away the sins of the earth. His very understanding of things is woven with the Catholic iconography of sacrifice and suffering. <br />The surrounding universe corroborates the place he sees for himself there. Lola tells him before she dies, "It‚Äôs just that blood seems like a kind of fertilizer in your life..." <br /><br />When his sister Rosario asks why he says he is damned, Pascual responds, "I‚Äôm not the one who says it." <br /><br />Such is the "real magicalism" of Cela, who paints the everyday gray, then drapes it in golden thread and lively luster. <br />In magical realism, the extraordinary is invited to accompany the ordinary on its daily rounds; in the real magicalism of Pascual‚Äôs mind, the very ordinary takes on the cast of something extraordinary by the projection of his fevered mind on the contrasting drabness and boredom of his surroundings <br /><br />Camilo Jos√© Cela's inspiration to write The Family of Pascual Duarte might be seen as having an intrinsic connection to his own colorful political life in Spain. Cela was born in May 1916 in Iria Flavia, Galicia; a province steeped in fog, drizzle and a black magic mythology to match them. <br /><br />In 1934, he began the study of medicine, but soon wound up under the tutelage of Pedro Salinas, a poet and member of the legendary "Generation of 1927" which counted, among its numbers, one of Spain‚Äôs most triumphant literary exports, Federico Garcia Lorca. <br /><br />Set up in Madrid‚Äôs well-heeled Barrio Salamanca at the outset of the Civil War, Cela signed on with the Fascist fighting units of General Millan Ashtray whose war cry was "Long Live Death!" This experience could explain Pascual's righteous obsession with death and murder. <br /><br />Later, Cela would serve for a time as a censor to the Franco regime only to see his own work receive the same rough treatment later. In 1974, he resigned his post as president of the prestigious Madrid Atheneum over the anarchist Salvador Puig Antich‚Äôs execution. It seems Cela was always living at odds with the power brokers of his time. In 1962, he dedicated the 13th edition of The Family of Pascual Duarte to his "enemies, who have been of such help to me in my career." <br /><br />Cela, by all accounts, was a man who planned to be famous, and to be so as a writer. He was prolific throughout his life, crafting internationally acclaimed novels, less critically adored plays, countless essays and articles. <br /><br />He won the Nobel Prize in 1989 and quickly turned it to his commercial advantage, developing what he himself considered "the business of Camilo Jos√© Cela." Until his death in 2002 he roamed the streets and bars of Madrid with his youngish wife, living the old-style literary life in a European capital, collecting caviar prizes and stipends, expounding in electronic and print media on any number of topics, contemporary and otherwise. <br /><br />In the years following Franco‚Äôs death he was disdained by the political right over his criticism of the Franco regime and reviled by the reigning cultural elites of the ruling Socialist Party. <br /><br />He could have cared less and if moved to, gave as good as he got. None of it could dent his hard-earned triumph, rooted more firmly in the quality and variety of his work than the meticulously crafted public persona he employed in shadowboxing the world around him. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18055830417470187600" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2157074982" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2157074982" />
    <a title="Bob Newman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/72341080-bob-newman"><img alt="Bob Newman" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2157074982?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bob Newman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bob Newman" href="/user/show/72341080-bob-newman">Bob Newman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72341080-bob-newman?shelf=africa">africa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72341080-bob-newman?shelf=european-literature">european-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2157074982" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8037100399895567193">Life Is Ugly"<br/>Not everybody can control themselves, that's true. With some people passions lie close to the surface, uncertainty hovers over them like a rain cloud, soaking their better impulses, encouraging gloom. It seems to me that most writers of great literature [and Cela is no doubt one] form characters who wish to do well and to be successful in whatever they choose, big or small in scope. They often fail. But this novel is among the minority in which evil is explained, ugly feelings and b</span>
  <span id="freeText8037100399895567193" style="display:none">Life Is Ugly"<br />Not everybody can control themselves, that's true. With some people passions lie close to the surface, uncertainty hovers over them like a rain cloud, soaking their better impulses, encouraging gloom. It seems to me that most writers of great literature [and Cela is no doubt one] form characters who wish to do well and to be successful in whatever they choose, big or small in scope. They often fail. But this novel is among the minority in which evil is explained, ugly feelings and brutal deeds made plain. While admitting that the writer is great, I found this novel extremely unpleasant. Not only does the main character, Pascual Duarte, commit three murders, but we see how a man dies locked in a closet when he gets rabies and how hogs chew off a retarded child's ears. Our "hero" also kills a dog and a horse. The overall tone reminded me, in its pessimistic, negative view of life, of Dostoevsky's "Notes from Underground", of Camus' "L'Etranger", the works of Gide, and certain works by Mishima and Tanizaki. There is almost no redeeming feature of human nature here. Suffering is nearly universal among people, but most do not become murderers. Looking around, historically and today, there's ample reason to feel hopeless when it comes to our species. So, if that attitude is what turns you on, I suppose this will be a 5-star book. While acknowledging the evil, vindictive side of Man, I prefer to read less concentrated "absence of good". Written by Pascual Duarte as he sits on Death Row in 1930s Spain, the chapter recall his entire life up to 1922. He is created by hardship, lovelessness, alcohol, violence, and his own great insecurity. It is not that it is surprising, rather it is the feeling I got throughout that the author was telling me, "This is real life. Anger, Blood, Violence are the Truth. So this is a real man." I don't agree.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8037100399895567193" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2139251579" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2139251579" />
    <a title="Esculapio Poblete" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70925092-esculapio-poblete"><img alt="Esculapio Poblete" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586614728p2/70925092.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2139251579?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Esculapio Poblete" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Esculapio Poblete" href="/user/show/70925092-esculapio-poblete">Esculapio Poblete</a>
      </span>

        rated it

  <span id="freeText9026503538383748898" style="display:none">This is a book to be read when you‚Äôre grown up. Too hard to understand, too subtle to grasp when you¬¥re young. Even so I would recommend anyone to read it, keep it close, and read it again when he felts it¬¥s ready to. <br /><br />I‚Äôm quite sure that I‚Äôm so fond of the story because I‚Äôve been able to assist first-hand the hardships of rural life. Not as harsh as this impoverished area of Spain, because the territory were the story takes place was a sad extreme example of backwardness and poverty.<br /><br />The novel takes form as a manuscript by the same Duarte. He‚Äôs living his last days, waiting for the dead penalty, and he engage his days filling in sheets of paper with his life‚Äôs story. You get to know the character because you have access to his inner thoughts. Even so, there is something in his figure that is beyond reaching. And you know it from the start, because the sad end of ‚ÄúChispa‚Äù, his beloved hunting dog.<br /><br />This was the first novel of Cela. He had published before a book of poems, but it was Duarte who give him status as writer. If in this book he chose the figure of the manuscript by the main character, Cela continued experimenting different techniques. It¬¥s from my point of view of extra value that he explained why and how he had chosen to tell the story in a way or another.<br /><br />The book prompted a film. I‚Äôve not seen it, but it¬¥s dubious that so much hardship can be taken to the viewer without upsetting it. Moreover, I cannot understand how so much of Duarte inner thoughts can be transformed into images.<br /></span>

  <span id="freeText17636169118333560575" style="display:none">Camilo Jos√© Cela Trulock was a Spaniard writer from Galicia. Prolific author (as a novelist, journalist, essayist, literary magazine editor, lecturer ...), he was a member of the Royal Spanish Academy for 45 years and won, among others, the Prince of Asturias Prize for Literature in 1987, the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1989 ("for a rich and intensive prose, which with restrained compassion forms a challenging vision of man's vulnerability.") and the Cervantes Prize in 1995.<br /><br />In 1996 King Juan Carlos I granted him, for his literary merits, the title Marquis of Iria Flavia.<br /><br />His son, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6461756.Camilo_Jos__Cela_Conde" title="Camilo Jos√© Cela Conde" rel="nofollow noopener">Camilo Jos√© Cela Conde</a> is also a writer.<br /><br />See also <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Camilo_Jos√©_Cela">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Camilo_J...</a></span>
